      <a class="d-inline-block" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/noirblue/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="/noirblue"><img class=" avatar-user" src="https://avatars.githubusercontent.com/u/29530003?s=80&amp;v=4" width="40" height="40" alt="@noirblue" /></a>
    </div>
    <div id="gistcomment-6171173" class="timeline-comment-group js-minimizable-comment-group js-targetable-element my-0 comment previewable-edit js-task-list-container js-comment timeline-comment timeline-comment--caret tmp-ml-n3 js-minimize-container unminimized-comment">
        <div class="timeline-comment-header clearfix d-flex flex-items-start"  data-morpheus-enabled="false">
  <div class="d-flex flex-row-reverse flex-items-center flex-shrink-0 tmp-pl-3">
    <div class="timeline-comment-actions flex-shrink-0 d-flex flex-items-center">
      <details class="details-overlay details-reset position-relative d-inline-block">
        <summary data-view-component="true" class="timeline-comment-action Link--secondary Button--link Button--medium Button">  <span class="Button-content">
    <span class="Button-label"><svg aria-label="Show options" role="img" data-component="Octicon" height="16" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" data-view-component="true" class="octicon octicon-kebab-horizontal">
    <path d="M8 9a1.5 1.5 0 1 0 0-3 1.5 1.5 0 0 0 0 3ZM1.5 9a1.5 1.5 0 1 0 0-3 1.5 1.5 0 0 0 0 3Zm13 0a1.5 1.5 0 1 0 0-3 1.5 1.5 0 0 0 0 3Z"></path>
</svg></span>
  </span>
</summary>

        <details-menu
          class="dropdown-menu dropdown-menu-sw show-more-popover color-fg-default"
          style="width:185px"
          src=""
          preload
          
        >
            <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy aria-label="Copy link" for="gistcomment-6171173-permalink" role="menuitem" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
              Copy link

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>              <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="Been running Karpathy&#39;s LLM-Wiki pattern for five weeks now.

The compounding effect is real. Drop a paper, get a cross-linked wiki page. Ask a question, get a cited answer that gets filed back into the graph. The maintenance burden is near zero because the LLM does the bookkeeping.

But — when I tried to scale it beyond markdown notes, I hit a wall.

PDFs needed PyMuPDF. Office docs needed python-docx. Video transcripts needed Whisper. Structured LLM outputs needed Instructor. Each tool had its own vault, its own SQLite schema, its own API surface. I built a 450-line Python glue layer to stitch them together.

The glue proved the problem: **fragmentation at the architecture layer.** Four vaults, four schemas, wikilink collisions, frontmatter drift, MCP namespace explosions. Glue is insufficient because it preserves the fragmentation; it just moves it around.

So I wrote a spec for unification at the architecture layer instead:

**Mnemosyne** — a local-first semantic memory OS with a Rust core and Python satellites.

- One vault: `raw/`, `wiki/`, `memory/`, `packs/`
- One schema: SQLite `state.db` with `pages`, `links`, `jobs`, `conversations`, `audit_log`, `contradictions`
- One API: MCP + REST + CLI from day one
- Rust core: schema, job queue, graph traversal, API surface
- Python satellites: PDF extraction, office docs, video/audio transcripts, LLM client glue, structured compilation
- Two-tier compilation: fast model (30B) extracts concepts, heavy model (70B+) writes cross-linked articles
- Audit engine: structural lint → contradiction detection → adversarial review
- Provenance-first: every claim carries citation, every LLM call carries cost log

The Python satellites are permanent, not transitional. Rust cannot yet match Python&#39;s depth in document extraction (pymupdf, python-docx, Whisper) or structured LLM output (Instructor, Pydantic-AI). The Rust core spawns them as subprocesses, receives JSON, and integrates into the unified system.

Status: architecture spec complete. Seeking core contributors for Phase 1 implementation (SQLite schema, vault init, markdown ingestion, basic MCP server).

Spec: https://github.com/noirblue/IsaacCLupus_mnemosyn_spec

If you&#39;ve hit the same fragmentation wall trying to scale the LLM-Wiki pattern, I&#39;d love to talk." data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
                Copy Markdown

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>        </details-menu>
      </details>
    </div>

    <div class="d-none d-sm-flex">
        

        

    </div>
  </div>

  <h3 class="f5 text-normal py-2" style="flex: 1 1 auto">
    <div class="d-flex flex-items-center flex-wrap gap-1">
      <span class="d-inline-flex">
          
      </span>

      <strong>
            <a class="author Link--primary text-bold css-overflow-wrap-anywhere " show_full_name="false" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/noirblue/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="/noirblue">noirblue</a>
  

      </strong>

      

      commented 


        <a href="/karpathy/442a6bf555914893e9891c11519de94f?permalink_comment_id=6171173#gistcomment-6171173" id="gistcomment-6171173-permalink" class="Link--secondary js-timestamp"><relative-time datetime="2026-05-27T16:57:09Z" class="no-wrap">May 27, 2026</relative-time></a>



      
    </div>

  </h3>
</div>


        <div class="edit-comment-hide">
  <task-lists disabled sortable>
    <div class="comment-body markdown-body js-comment-body soft-wrap user-select-contain d-block">
      <p dir="auto">Been running Karpathy's LLM-Wiki pattern for five weeks now.</p>
<p dir="auto">The compounding effect is real. Drop a paper, get a cross-linked wiki page. Ask a question, get a cited answer that gets filed back into the graph. The maintenance burden is near zero because the LLM does the bookkeeping.</p>
<p dir="auto">But — when I tried to scale it beyond markdown notes, I hit a wall.</p>
<p dir="auto">PDFs needed PyMuPDF. Office docs needed python-docx. Video transcripts needed Whisper. Structured LLM outputs needed Instructor. Each tool had its own vault, its own SQLite schema, its own API surface. I built a 450-line Python glue layer to stitch them together.</p>
<p dir="auto">The glue proved the problem: <strong>fragmentation at the architecture layer.</strong> Four vaults, four schemas, wikilink collisions, frontmatter drift, MCP namespace explosions. Glue is insufficient because it preserves the fragmentation; it just moves it around.</p>
<p dir="auto">So I wrote a spec for unification at the architecture layer instead:</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Mnemosyne</strong> — a local-first semantic memory OS with a Rust core and Python satellites.</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>One vault: <code class="notranslate">raw/</code>, <code class="notranslate">wiki/</code>, <code class="notranslate">memory/</code>, <code class="notranslate">packs/</code></li>
<li>One schema: SQLite <code class="notranslate">state.db</code> with <code class="notranslate">pages</code>, <code class="notranslate">links</code>, <code class="notranslate">jobs</code>, <code class="notranslate">conversations</code>, <code class="notranslate">audit_log</code>, <code class="notranslate">contradictions</code></li>
<li>One API: MCP + REST + CLI from day one</li>
<li>Rust core: schema, job queue, graph traversal, API surface</li>
<li>Python satellites: PDF extraction, office docs, video/audio transcripts, LLM client glue, structured compilation</li>
<li>Two-tier compilation: fast model (30B) extracts concepts, heavy model (70B+) writes cross-linked articles</li>
<li>Audit engine: structural lint → contradiction detection → adversarial review</li>
<li>Provenance-first: every claim carries citation, every LLM call carries cost log</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">The Python satellites are permanent, not transitional. Rust cannot yet match Python's depth in document extraction (pymupdf, python-docx, Whisper) or structured LLM output (Instructor, Pydantic-AI). The Rust core spawns them as subprocesses, receives JSON, and integrates into the unified system.</p>
<p dir="auto">Status: architecture spec complete. Seeking core contributors for Phase 1 implementation (SQLite schema, vault init, markdown ingestion, basic MCP server).</p>
<p dir="auto">Spec: <a href="https://github.com/noirblue/IsaacCLupus_mnemosyn_spec">https://github.com/noirblue/IsaacCLupus_mnemosyn_spec</a></p>
<p dir="auto">If you've hit the same fragmentation wall trying to scale the LLM-Wiki pattern, I'd love to talk.</p>
    </div>
  </task-lists>
  
</div>

            <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy aria-label="Copy link" for="gistcomment-6171816-permalink" role="menuitem" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
              Copy link

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>              <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="&lt;img width=&quot;100%&quot; alt=&quot;claude-obsidian: a self-organizing AI wiki for Obsidian + Claude Code&quot; src=&quot;https://agricidaniel.com/images/blog/claude-obsidian-cover.webp&quot; /&gt;

This pattern crystallized something a lot of us were circling. I ended up building a full open-source implementation of it as my daily driver: **[claude-obsidian](https://github.com/AgriciDaniel/claude-obsidian)** (MIT, runs in Claude Code). Same three layers you describe, raw → wiki → schema, with `index.md` for navigation and an append-only `log.md`, and the model maintains the wiki while you pick sources and ask questions.

Here are two real vaults it built, the graph is generated, not hand-linked:

| | |
|---|---|
| ![Dense generated knowledge graph](https://claude-blog.md/assets/blog/claude-obsidian/co-vault-graph-a.webp) | ![Vault folder structure and graph](https://claude-blog.md/assets/blog/claude-obsidian/co-vault-graph-b.webp) |

A few questions in this thread are exactly the ones it tries to answer:

- **New page vs. edit an existing one** (@alinawab): on each ingest it extracts entities/concepts, searches the existing vault first, and edits + cross-links on a match, only creating a page when nothing fits. Wikilinks and contradiction flags are added automatically.
- **Where it starts fighting you**: the one that bit me was *concurrent writes* (two agents ingesting at once could corrupt a page mid-write); v1.7 added per-file locking to close that. The other is retrieval drift on big vaults, handled with optional hybrid retrieval (contextual prefix + BM25 + rerank, per Anthropic&#39;s contextual-retrieval work).
- **Sharing with a team** (@geetansharora): it&#39;s just plain Markdown in a git repo, no DB or MCP server required, so a team shares it like code, clone the vault and everyone&#39;s Claude reads the same files.

Free and MIT. Two-minute walkthrough + repo:
- 🎥 Video: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=I2hXQ-_qzRk
- ⭐ Repo: https://github.com/AgriciDaniel/claude-obsidian
- 📝 Latest release (v1.9 &quot;Compound Vault&quot;): https://github.com/AgriciDaniel/claude-obsidian/releases/tag/v1.9.2

Thanks for the writeup, @karpathy, it&#39;s shaped how I think about this.
" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
                Copy Markdown

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>        </details-menu>
      </details>

      <strong>
            <a class="author Link--primary text-bold css-overflow-wrap-anywhere " show_full_name="false" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/AgriciDaniel/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="/AgriciDaniel">AgriciDaniel</a>

        <div class="edit-comment-hide">
  <task-lists disabled sortable>
    <div class="comment-body markdown-body js-comment-body soft-wrap user-select-contain d-block">
      <p dir="auto"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer nofollow" href="https://camo.githubusercontent.com/6dd42df25dfaa18b01b2256b9115bdc037ad36138095b226395f75f1fc5ad3b6/68747470733a2f2f61677269636964616e69656c2e636f6d2f696d616765732f626c6f672f636c617564652d6f6273696469616e2d636f7665722e77656270"><img width="100%" alt="claude-obsidian: a self-organizing AI wiki for Obsidian + Claude Code" src="https://camo.githubusercontent.com/6dd42df25dfaa18b01b2256b9115bdc037ad36138095b226395f75f1fc5ad3b6/68747470733a2f2f61677269636964616e69656c2e636f6d2f696d616765732f626c6f672f636c617564652d6f6273696469616e2d636f7665722e77656270" data-canonical-src="https://agricidaniel.com/images/blog/claude-obsidian-cover.webp" style="max-width: 100%;"></a></p>
<p dir="auto">This pattern crystallized something a lot of us were circling. I ended up building a full open-source implementation of it as my daily driver: <strong><a href="https://github.com/AgriciDaniel/claude-obsidian">claude-obsidian</a></strong> (MIT, runs in Claude Code). Same three layers you describe, raw → wiki → schema, with <code class="notranslate">index.md</code> for navigation and an append-only <code class="notranslate">log.md</code>, and the model maintains the wiki while you pick sources and ask questions.</p>
<p dir="auto">Here are two real vaults it built, the graph is generated, not hand-linked:</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table role="table">
<thead>
<tr>
<th></th>
<th></th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><a target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer nofollow" href="https://camo.githubusercontent.com/7ac2a3b3f535103f9415f5b82413f8ad894b1cb72d7a2e91579f512321cd6954/68747470733a2f2f636c617564652d626c6f672e6d642f6173736574732f626c6f672f636c617564652d6f6273696469616e2f636f2d7661756c742d67726170682d612e77656270"><img src="https://camo.githubusercontent.com/7ac2a3b3f535103f9415f5b82413f8ad894b1cb72d7a2e91579f512321cd6954/68747470733a2f2f636c617564652d626c6f672e6d642f6173736574732f626c6f672f636c617564652d6f6273696469616e2f636f2d7661756c742d67726170682d612e77656270" alt="Dense generated knowledge graph" data-canonical-src="https://claude-blog.md/assets/blog/claude-obsidian/co-vault-graph-a.webp" style="max-width: 100%;"></a></td>
<td><a target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer nofollow" href="https://camo.githubusercontent.com/d5a7860832fbc2d252993e8f1a1d62414ebbd2dfefb9e3cc9489b5bac5b8a083/68747470733a2f2f636c617564652d626c6f672e6d642f6173736574732f626c6f672f636c617564652d6f6273696469616e2f636f2d7661756c742d67726170682d622e77656270"><img src="https://camo.githubusercontent.com/d5a7860832fbc2d252993e8f1a1d62414ebbd2dfefb9e3cc9489b5bac5b8a083/68747470733a2f2f636c617564652d626c6f672e6d642f6173736574732f626c6f672f636c617564652d6f6273696469616e2f636f2d7661756c742d67726170682d622e77656270" alt="Vault folder structure and graph" data-canonical-src="https://claude-blog.md/assets/blog/claude-obsidian/co-vault-graph-b.webp" style="max-width: 100%;"></a></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">A few questions in this thread are exactly the ones it tries to answer:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><strong>New page vs. edit an existing one</strong> (<a class="user-mention notranslate" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/alinawab/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="https://github.com/alinawab">@alinawab</a>): on each ingest it extracts entities/concepts, searches the existing vault first, and edits + cross-links on a match, only creating a page when nothing fits. Wikilinks and contradiction flags are added automatically.</li>
<li><strong>Where it starts fighting you</strong>: the one that bit me was <em>concurrent writes</em> (two agents ingesting at once could corrupt a page mid-write); v1.7 added per-file locking to close that. The other is retrieval drift on big vaults, handled with optional hybrid retrieval (contextual prefix + BM25 + rerank, per Anthropic's contextual-retrieval work).</li>
<li><strong>Sharing with a team</strong> (<a class="user-mention notranslate" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/geetansharora/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="https://github.com/geetansharora">@geetansharora</a>): it's just plain Markdown in a git repo, no DB or MCP server required, so a team shares it like code, clone the vault and everyone's Claude reads the same files.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Free and MIT. Two-minute walkthrough + repo:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>🎥 Video: <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=I2hXQ-_qzRk" rel="nofollow">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=I2hXQ-_qzRk</a></li>
<li>⭐ Repo: <a href="https://github.com/AgriciDaniel/claude-obsidian">https://github.com/AgriciDaniel/claude-obsidian</a></li>
<li>📝 Latest release (v1.9 "Compound Vault"): <a href="https://github.com/AgriciDaniel/claude-obsidian/releases/tag/v1.9.2">https://github.com/AgriciDaniel/claude-obsidian/releases/tag/v1.9.2</a></li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Thanks for the writeup, <a class="user-mention notranslate" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/karpathy/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="https://github.com/karpathy">@karpathy</a>, it's shaped how I think about this.</p>
    </div>
  </task-lists>
  
</div>

  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="Thanks for this write-up, @karpathy. It helped me connect and contextualize many years of personal OneNote documentation that had accumulated as scattered knowledge.

I have also started applying this idea to a fictional manufacturing plant use case: a web interface that ingests operating, maintenance, engineering and troubleshooting documents that normally reside in different places and must be manually pieced together when a plant problem occurs.

The aim is to create an evolving knowledge layer that helps users connect information across documents during troubleshooting, while allowing them to select and run local LLM models of their choice.

For industrial applications, this approach is especially valuable because useful knowledge is rarely contained in one document; it emerges from the relationships among operating history, equipment records, procedures, drawings and past decisions.

    <div class="comment-body markdown-body js-comment-body soft-wrap user-select-contain d-block">
      <p dir="auto">Thanks for this write-up, <a class="user-mention notranslate" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/karpathy/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="https://github.com/karpathy">@karpathy</a>. It helped me connect and contextualize many years of personal OneNote documentation that had accumulated as scattered knowledge.</p>
<p dir="auto">I have also started applying this idea to a fictional manufacturing plant use case: a web interface that ingests operating, maintenance, engineering and troubleshooting documents that normally reside in different places and must be manually pieced together when a plant problem occurs.</p>
<p dir="auto">The aim is to create an evolving knowledge layer that helps users connect information across documents during troubleshooting, while allowing them to select and run local LLM models of their choice.</p>
<p dir="auto">For industrial applications, this approach is especially valuable because useful knowledge is rarely contained in one document; it emerges from the relationships among operating history, equipment records, procedures, drawings and past decisions.<br>
<a target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://private-user-images.githubusercontent.com/41238478/599390745-ec513d94-d939-43c0-b8fa-8a4381ccee26.png?jwt=eyJ0eXAiOiJKV1QiLCJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiJ9.eyJpc3MiOiJnaXRodWIuY29tIiwiYXVkIjoicmF3LmdpdGh1YnVzZXJjb250ZW50LmNvbSIsImtleSI6ImtleTUiLCJleHAiOjE3ODAyODYzMDgsIm5iZiI6MTc4MDI4NjAwOCwicGF0aCI6Ii80MTIzODQ3OC81OTkzOTA3NDUtZWM1MTNkOTQtZDkzOS00M2MwLWI4ZmEtOGE0MzgxY2NlZTI2LnBuZz9YLUFtei1BbGdvcml0aG09QVdTNC1ITUFDLVNIQTI1NiZYLUFtei1DcmVkZW50aWFsPUFLSUFWQ09EWUxTQTUzUFFLNFpBJTJGMjAyNjA2MDElMkZ1cy1lYXN0LTElMkZzMyUyRmF3czRfcmVxdWVzdCZYLUFtei1EYXRlPTIwMjYwNjAxVDAzNTMyOFomWC1BbXotRXhwaXJlcz0zMDAmWC1BbXotU2lnbmF0dXJlPThmZjI3ZjNlZTc3NWI0NjA4ODliMTkzYmI4ZDMyMzc2Y2QzYmYyNmE3ZjA5ZmJlYzNhZjY0ZjQ1NTNkMzU4NDAmWC1BbXotU2lnbmVkSGVhZGVycz1ob3N0JnJlc3BvbnNlLWNvbnRlbnQtdHlwZT1pbWFnZSUyRnBuZyJ9.r34eGi_FcPDzxyD9Y4mtRADwJ-M1l68unqJBg5exZkA"><img width="834" height="693" alt="plant_wiki_controlPanel" src="https://private-user-images.githubusercontent.com/41238478/599390745-ec513d94-d939-43c0-b8fa-8a4381ccee26.png?jwt=eyJ0eXAiOiJKV1QiLCJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiJ9.eyJpc3MiOiJnaXRodWIuY29tIiwiYXVkIjoicmF3LmdpdGh1YnVzZXJjb250ZW50LmNvbSIsImtleSI6ImtleTUiLCJleHAiOjE3ODAyODYzMDgsIm5iZiI6MTc4MDI4NjAwOCwicGF0aCI6Ii80MTIzODQ3OC81OTkzOTA3NDUtZWM1MTNkOTQtZDkzOS00M2MwLWI4ZmEtOGE0MzgxY2NlZTI2LnBuZz9YLUFtei1BbGdvcml0aG09QVdTNC1ITUFDLVNIQTI1NiZYLUFtei1DcmVkZW50aWFsPUFLSUFWQ09EWUxTQTUzUFFLNFpBJTJGMjAyNjA2MDElMkZ1cy1lYXN0LTElMkZzMyUyRmF3czRfcmVxdWVzdCZYLUFtei1EYXRlPTIwMjYwNjAxVDAzNTMyOFomWC1BbXotRXhwaXJlcz0zMDAmWC1BbXotU2lnbmF0dXJlPThmZjI3ZjNlZTc3NWI0NjA4ODliMTkzYmI4ZDMyMzc2Y2QzYmYyNmE3ZjA5ZmJlYzNhZjY0ZjQ1NTNkMzU4NDAmWC1BbXotU2lnbmVkSGVhZGVycz1ob3N0JnJlc3BvbnNlLWNvbnRlbnQtdHlwZT1pbWFnZSUyRnBuZyJ9.r34eGi_FcPDzxyD9Y4mtRADwJ-M1l68unqJBg5exZkA" style="max-width: 100%; height: auto; max-height: 693px;; aspect-ratio: 834 / 693; background-color: var(--bgColor-muted); border-radius: 6px" class="js-gh-image-fallback"></a><br>

    <div id="gistcomment-6172540" class="timeline-comment-group js-minimizable-comment-group js-targetable-element my-0 comment previewable-edit js-task-list-container js-comment timeline-comment timeline-comment--caret tmp-ml-n3 js-minimize-container unminimized-comment">

    <span class="Button-label"><svg aria-label="Show options" role="img" data-component="Octicon" height="16" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" data-view-component="true" class="octicon octicon-kebab-horizontal">
    <path d="M8 9a1.5 1.5 0 1 0 0-3 1.5 1.5 0 0 0 0 3ZM1.5 9a1.5 1.5 0 1 0 0-3 1.5 1.5 0 0 0 0 3Zm13 0a1.5 1.5 0 1 0 0-3 1.5 1.5 0 0 0 0 3Z"></path>

          style="width:185px"
          src=""
          preload

            <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy aria-label="Copy link" for="gistcomment-6172540-permalink" role="menuitem" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
              Copy link

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>              <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="https://github.com/alirezabbasi/echel/releases/tag/v0.2.0

I just released **Echel v0.2.0**.

Echel started from the same core insight behind Andrej Karpathy’s “LLM Wiki” idea: AI workflows should not keep rediscovering knowledge from scratch every session.

The LLM Wiki pattern says: instead of relying only on RAG or chat history, let AI continuously maintain a structured, interlinked wiki that compounds over time.

Echel takes that idea into software product creation.

It is a platform where a domain expert defines a problem, intended solution, constraints, risks, MVP, stack, and direction, and Echel continuously turns that intent into:

- clarified requirements
- product architecture
- roadmap
- executable work
- graph-backed AI agent packets
- review reports
- readiness checks
- proof packs
- compounding project intelligence

The key idea is simple:

**The product should not lose memory every time the AI context window resets.**

In v0.2.0, Echel now includes:

- product-first initialization
- root-level product `wiki/`
- internal `echel-core/` runtime split
- product-owner commands like `define`, `clarify`, `plan`, `build`, `review`, `steer`, and `status`
- typed product intelligence graph
- agent work packets
- product cockpit
- milestone/readiness gates
- proof packs and release summaries

The direction is to make Echel a product-creation operating system for AI-native development.

A business owner or domain expert should be able to steer the product through intent and decisions, while Echel and AI agents handle the structured execution loop.

Huge inspiration from the LLM Wiki pattern:
https://gist.github.com/karpathy/442a6bf555914893e9891c11519de94f

Echel v0.2.0 is the first version where that idea becomes a fuller software-development workflow:

**intent -&gt; memory -&gt; graph -&gt; plan -&gt; build packet -&gt; implementation -&gt; review -&gt; readiness -&gt; updated intelligence**" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
                Copy Markdown

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>        </details-menu>
      </details>
    </div>

<p dir="auto">I just released <strong>Echel v0.2.0</strong>.</p>
<p dir="auto">Echel started from the same core insight behind Andrej Karpathy’s “LLM Wiki” idea: AI workflows should not keep rediscovering knowledge from scratch every session.</p>
<p dir="auto">The LLM Wiki pattern says: instead of relying only on RAG or chat history, let AI continuously maintain a structured, interlinked wiki that compounds over time.</p>
<p dir="auto">Echel takes that idea into software product creation.</p>
<p dir="auto">It is a platform where a domain expert defines a problem, intended solution, constraints, risks, MVP, stack, and direction, and Echel continuously turns that intent into:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>clarified requirements</li>
<li>product architecture</li>
<li>roadmap</li>
<li>executable work</li>
<li>graph-backed AI agent packets</li>
<li>review reports</li>
<li>readiness checks</li>
<li>proof packs</li>
<li>compounding project intelligence</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">The key idea is simple:</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>The product should not lose memory every time the AI context window resets.</strong></p>
<p dir="auto">In v0.2.0, Echel now includes:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>product-first initialization</li>
<li>root-level product <code class="notranslate">wiki/</code></li>
<li>internal <code class="notranslate">echel-core/</code> runtime split</li>
<li>product-owner commands like <code class="notranslate">define</code>, <code class="notranslate">clarify</code>, <code class="notranslate">plan</code>, <code class="notranslate">build</code>, <code class="notranslate">review</code>, <code class="notranslate">steer</code>, and <code class="notranslate">status</code></li>
<li>typed product intelligence graph</li>
<li>agent work packets</li>
<li>product cockpit</li>
<li>milestone/readiness gates</li>
<li>proof packs and release summaries</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">The direction is to make Echel a product-creation operating system for AI-native development.</p>
<p dir="auto">A business owner or domain expert should be able to steer the product through intent and decisions, while Echel and AI agents handle the structured execution loop.</p>
<p dir="auto">Huge inspiration from the LLM Wiki pattern:<br>
<a href="https://gist.github.com/karpathy/442a6bf555914893e9891c11519de94f">https://gist.github.com/karpathy/442a6bf555914893e9891c11519de94f</a></p>
<p dir="auto">Echel v0.2.0 is the first version where that idea becomes a fuller software-development workflow:</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>intent -&gt; memory -&gt; graph -&gt; plan -&gt; build packet -&gt; implementation -&gt; review -&gt; readiness -&gt; updated intelligence</strong></p>

  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="Built a lint extension to combat ingestion order bias in wikis with 50+ sources: [LLM Wiki: A Bias-Aware Stateful Lint](https://gist.github.com/brtrx/ba595fd01e344d797cb66d34d982ecad)

Two problems emerge at scale — context limits kill single-pass lint, and ingestion order biases the wiki toward earlier sources. The extension runs lint in batches of 5 with a persistent scratchpad across sessions, and uses a randomised (not ingestion-order) source sequence to surface cross-cutting contradictions that ordered passes miss.

Drops into an existing CLAUDE.md. Doesn&#39;t fix framing bias baked in at ingest time, but repairs the structural layer reliably." data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">

        <div class="edit-comment-hide">
  <task-lists disabled sortable>
    <div class="comment-body markdown-body js-comment-body soft-wrap user-select-contain d-block">
      <p dir="auto">Built a lint extension to combat ingestion order bias in wikis with 50+ sources: <a href="https://gist.github.com/brtrx/ba595fd01e344d797cb66d34d982ecad">LLM Wiki: A Bias-Aware Stateful Lint</a></p>
<p dir="auto">Two problems emerge at scale — context limits kill single-pass lint, and ingestion order biases the wiki toward earlier sources. The extension runs lint in batches of 5 with a persistent scratchpad across sessions, and uses a randomised (not ingestion-order) source sequence to surface cross-cutting contradictions that ordered passes miss.</p>
<p dir="auto">Drops into an existing CLAUDE.md. Doesn't fix framing bias baked in at ingest time, but repairs the structural layer reliably.</p>
    </div>
  </task-lists>

            <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy aria-label="Copy link" for="gistcomment-6172973-permalink" role="menuitem" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>              <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="A few people have asked: isn&#39;t this just a wiki compiler with extra steps?

No. Wiki compilation is one layer of seven. The broader goal is a **knowledge lifecycle**, not just ingestion:

- **Agent memory:** `memory/inbox/` → `memory/committed/` — propose, review, link, decay
- **Conversation history:** token-budgeted, cross-session, queryable
- **Dual-node deployment:** Foundry (batch compile/audit) + Frontline (interactive serve)
- **Audit as infrastructure:** every claim traced to source, every LLM call logged with cost/hash
- **Pack exports:** agent-ready subsets of the graph for action

The architecture uses a **Rust core** for the performance-critical path — schema, job queue, graph traversal, API surface — while Python satellites handle document extraction and LLM orchestration where Python&#39;s ecosystem has no equal. The split is permanent, not transitional.

The LLM-Wiki pattern proved that compounding works. Mnemosyne asks: what happens when you treat that pattern as **infrastructure** — with schemas, queues, provenance, and deployment topologies — rather than as content in markdown files?

Spec: https://github.com/noirblue/IsaacCLupus_mnemosyn_spec" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
                Copy Markdown

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>        </details-menu>
      </details>
    </div>

    <div class="d-none d-sm-flex">
        

        

    </div>
  </div>

            <a class="author Link--primary text-bold css-overflow-wrap-anywhere " show_full_name="false" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/noirblue/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="/noirblue">noirblue</a>
  

      </strong>

        <div class="edit-comment-hide">
  <task-lists disabled sortable>
    <div class="comment-body markdown-body js-comment-body soft-wrap user-select-contain d-block">
      <p dir="auto">A few people have asked: isn't this just a wiki compiler with extra steps?</p>
<p dir="auto">No. Wiki compilation is one layer of seven. The broader goal is a <strong>knowledge lifecycle</strong>, not just ingestion:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><strong>Agent memory:</strong> <code class="notranslate">memory/inbox/</code> → <code class="notranslate">memory/committed/</code> — propose, review, link, decay</li>
<li><strong>Conversation history:</strong> token-budgeted, cross-session, queryable</li>
<li><strong>Dual-node deployment:</strong> Foundry (batch compile/audit) + Frontline (interactive serve)</li>
<li><strong>Audit as infrastructure:</strong> every claim traced to source, every LLM call logged with cost/hash</li>
<li><strong>Pack exports:</strong> agent-ready subsets of the graph for action</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">The architecture uses a <strong>Rust core</strong> for the performance-critical path — schema, job queue, graph traversal, API surface — while Python satellites handle document extraction and LLM orchestration where Python's ecosystem has no equal. The split is permanent, not transitional.</p>
<p dir="auto">The LLM-Wiki pattern proved that compounding works. Mnemosyne asks: what happens when you treat that pattern as <strong>infrastructure</strong> — with schemas, queues, provenance, and deployment topologies — rather than as content in markdown files?</p>
<p dir="auto">Spec: <a href="https://github.com/noirblue/IsaacCLupus_mnemosyn_spec">https://github.com/noirblue/IsaacCLupus_mnemosyn_spec</a></p>
    </div>
  </task-lists>
  
</div>

  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="我按照 Karpathy 的思路开发了一个日记库，充分运用 Karpathy 的 **&quot;固定协议 + 可插拔模块&quot;** 架构。

用在自我认知上，比用在代码上更让人震撼。

**CLAUDE.md** 作为行为协议来约束 AI，而不是堆提示词。把 AI 从一个&quot;聊天对象&quot;变成了一只严格执行 SOP 的 analytic engine。

同时采用 **协议和人格分离** 的设计：协议保证行为可预期、可迁移（换 AI 客户端也不崩），人格随时换文件就能换风格——这是系统工程思维在个人管理上的降维打击。

日记架构按照 **L0 → L4 的分层深化**：

- 大多数日记工具停留在 L0（记录）
- 好一点的做到 L1（周回顾）

而我把跨时间复利对比（L3 / L4）直接做成内建功能。当日记从&quot;散装存档&quot;变成&quot;可检索的认知档案&quot;，它就不再是日记了——**是一个人的增量自我进化系统**。

👉 https://github.com/iddingszhz/Life_Daliy_OS" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">

      <p dir="auto">我按照 Karpathy 的思路开发了一个日记库，充分运用 Karpathy 的 <strong>"固定协议 + 可插拔模块"</strong> 架构。</p>
<p dir="auto">用在自我认知上，比用在代码上更让人震撼。</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>CLAUDE.md</strong> 作为行为协议来约束 AI，而不是堆提示词。把 AI 从一个"聊天对象"变成了一只严格执行 SOP 的 analytic engine。</p>
<p dir="auto">同时采用 <strong>协议和人格分离</strong> 的设计：协议保证行为可预期、可迁移（换 AI 客户端也不崩），人格随时换文件就能换风格——这是系统工程思维在个人管理上的降维打击。</p>
<p dir="auto">日记架构按照 <strong>L0 → L4 的分层深化</strong>：</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>大多数日记工具停留在 L0（记录）</li>
<li>好一点的做到 L1（周回顾）</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">而我把跨时间复利对比（L3 / L4）直接做成内建功能。当日记从"散装存档"变成"可检索的认知档案"，它就不再是日记了——<strong>是一个人的增量自我进化系统</strong>。</p>
<p dir="auto">👉 <a href="https://github.com/iddingszhz/Life_Daliy_OS">https://github.com/iddingszhz/Life_Daliy_OS</a></p>

  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="Synthadoc v0.6.0 is out.

Two new features focused on content health and portability:

1. Five-State Page Lifecycle: pages now move through five states: draft → active → stale → contradicted → archived. Transitions are mostly automatic: a clean lint pass promotes draft to active; a SHA-256 hash mismatch on the source file marks the page stale; ingesting a conflicting source marks it contradicted. Every transition is permanently logged with who triggered it, when, and why, so you have an auditable trail of when content was reviewed, not just who last touched the file. Manual overrides (lifecycle activate, archive, restore) are there when automation isn&#39;t enough.

  → Quick-start demo - Step 8 (https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-8--manage-page-lifecycle)


2. Wiki Export: four formats in one command: synthadoc export -f &lt;fmt&gt;. llms.txt and llms-full.txt follow the llmstxt.org spec for feeding the wiki into downstream LLM workflows. &quot;graphml&quot; exports the wikilink graph with node attributes (status, confidence, citation count) for analysis in Gephi or yEd.  &quot;json&quot; export includes three fields no generic wiki export produces: every paragraph traces back to exact source lines (claims[] -file name plus line range, not just &quot;from document X&quot;); the complete state-transition audit log (lifecycle_history[] - when the page was drafted, reviewed, went stale, re-ingested); and the per-page API cost to compile it (ingest_cost_usd).

  → Quick-start demo - Step 21 (https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-21--export-your-wiki)


3. Also shipping in v0.6.0: refined Obsidian plugin UX across lifecycle and export modals.

 
If any of this is useful or you have thoughts on the direction, feedback is very welcome, and a ⭐ always helps the project reach more people.

  👉 https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
                Copy Markdown

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>        </details-menu>
      </details>
    </div>

            <a class="author Link--primary text-bold css-overflow-wrap-anywhere " show_full_name="false" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/paulmchen/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="/paulmchen">paulmchen</a>

    </div>

        <div class="edit-comment-hide">
  <task-lists disabled sortable>
    <div class="comment-body markdown-body js-comment-body soft-wrap user-select-contain d-block">
      <p dir="auto">Synthadoc v0.6.0 is out.</p>
<p dir="auto">Two new features focused on content health and portability:</p>
<ol dir="auto">
<li>Five-State Page Lifecycle: pages now move through five states: draft → active → stale → contradicted → archived. Transitions are mostly automatic: a clean lint pass promotes draft to active; a SHA-256 hash mismatch on the source file marks the page stale; ingesting a conflicting source marks it contradicted. Every transition is permanently logged with who triggered it, when, and why, so you have an auditable trail of when content was reviewed, not just who last touched the file. Manual overrides (lifecycle activate, archive, restore) are there when automation isn't enough.</li>
</ol>
<p dir="auto">→ Quick-start demo - Step 8 (<a href="https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-8--manage-page-lifecycle">https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-8--manage-page-lifecycle</a>)</p>
<ol start="2" dir="auto">
<li>Wiki Export: four formats in one command: synthadoc export -f . llms.txt and llms-full.txt follow the llmstxt.org spec for feeding the wiki into downstream LLM workflows. "graphml" exports the wikilink graph with node attributes (status, confidence, citation count) for analysis in Gephi or yEd.  "json" export includes three fields no generic wiki export produces: every paragraph traces back to exact source lines (claims[] -file name plus line range, not just "from document X"); the complete state-transition audit log (lifecycle_history[] - when the page was drafted, reviewed, went stale, re-ingested); and the per-page API cost to compile it (ingest_cost_usd).</li>
</ol>
<p dir="auto">→ Quick-start demo - Step 21 (<a href="https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-21--export-your-wiki">https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-21--export-your-wiki</a>)</p>
<ol start="3" dir="auto">
<li>Also shipping in v0.6.0: refined Obsidian plugin UX across lifecycle and export modals.</li>
</ol>
<p dir="auto">If any of this is useful or you have thoughts on the direction, feedback is very welcome, and a ⭐ always helps the project reach more people.</p>
<p dir="auto">👉 <a href="https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc">https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc</a></p>
    </div>
  </task-lists>

On Fri, May 29, 2026 at 8:31 AM Paul Chen ***@***.***&gt; wrote:

&gt; ***@***.**** commented on this gist.
&gt; ------------------------------
&gt;
&gt; Synthadoc v0.6.0 is out.
&gt;
&gt; Two new features focused on content health and portability:
&gt;
&gt;    1. Five-State Page Lifecycle: pages now move through five states:
&gt;    draft → active → stale → contradicted → archived. Transitions are mostly
&gt;    automatic: a clean lint pass promotes draft to active; a SHA-256 hash
&gt;    mismatch on the source file marks the page stale; ingesting a conflicting
&gt;    source marks it contradicted. Every transition is permanently logged with
&gt;    who triggered it, when, and why, so you have an auditable trail of when
&gt;    content was reviewed, not just who last touched the file. Manual overrides
&gt;    (lifecycle activate, archive, restore) are there when automation isn&#39;t
&gt;    enough.
&gt;
&gt; → Quick-start demo - Step 8 (
&gt; https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-8--manage-page-lifecycle
&gt; )
&gt;
&gt;    2. Wiki Export: four formats in one command: synthadoc export -f .
&gt;    llms.txt and llms-full.txt follow the llmstxt.org spec for feeding the
&gt;    wiki into downstream LLM workflows. &quot;graphml&quot; exports the wikilink graph
&gt;    with node attributes (status, confidence, citation count) for analysis in
&gt;    Gephi or yEd. &quot;json&quot; export includes three fields no generic wiki export
&gt;    produces: every paragraph traces back to exact source lines (claims[] -file
&gt;    name plus line range, not just &quot;from document X&quot;); the complete
&gt;    state-transition audit log (lifecycle_history[] - when the page was
&gt;    drafted, reviewed, went stale, re-ingested); and the per-page API cost to
&gt;    compile it (ingest_cost_usd).
&gt;
&gt; → Quick-start demo - Step 21 (
&gt; https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-21--export-your-wiki
&gt; )
&gt;
&gt;    3. Also shipping in v0.6.0: refined Obsidian plugin UX across
&gt;    lifecycle and export modals.
&gt;
&gt; If any of this is useful or you have thoughts on the direction, feedback
&gt; is very welcome, and a ⭐ always helps the project reach more people.
&gt;
&gt; 👉 https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc
&gt;

<span class="email-hidden-toggle"><a href="#">…</a></span><div class="email-hidden-reply">
<div class="email-quoted-reply">On Fri, May 29, 2026 at 8:31 AM Paul Chen ***@***.***&gt; wrote:
 ***@***.**** commented on this gist.
 ------------------------------

 Synthadoc v0.6.0 is out.

 Two new features focused on content health and portability:

    1. Five-State Page Lifecycle: pages now move through five states:
    draft → active → stale → contradicted → archived. Transitions are mostly
    automatic: a clean lint pass promotes draft to active; a SHA-256 hash
    mismatch on the source file marks the page stale; ingesting a conflicting
    source marks it contradicted. Every transition is permanently logged with
    who triggered it, when, and why, so you have an auditable trail of when
    content was reviewed, not just who last touched the file. Manual overrides
    (lifecycle activate, archive, restore) are there when automation isn't
    enough.

 → Quick-start demo - Step 8 (
 <a href="https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-8--manage-page-lifecycle">https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-8--manage-page-lifecycle</a>
 )

    2. Wiki Export: four formats in one command: synthadoc export -f .
    llms.txt and llms-full.txt follow the llmstxt.org spec for feeding the
    wiki into downstream LLM workflows. "graphml" exports the wikilink graph
    with node attributes (status, confidence, citation count) for analysis in
    Gephi or yEd. "json" export includes three fields no generic wiki export
    produces: every paragraph traces back to exact source lines (claims[] -file
    name plus line range, not just "from document X"); the complete
    state-transition audit log (lifecycle_history[] - when the page was
    drafted, reviewed, went stale, re-ingested); and the per-page API cost to
    compile it (ingest_cost_usd).

 → Quick-start demo - Step 21 (
 <a href="https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-21--export-your-wiki">https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc/blob/main/docs/user-quick-start-guide.md#step-21--export-your-wiki</a>
 )

    3. Also shipping in v0.6.0: refined Obsidian plugin UX across
    lifecycle and export modals.

 If any of this is useful or you have thoughts on the direction, feedback
 is very welcome, and a ⭐ always helps the project reach more people.

 👉 <a href="https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc">https://github.com/axoviq-ai/synthadoc</a>

 —

</span>              <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="  This wiki pattern worked so well for *knowledge* that I tried the same
  plain-markdown-over-LLM idea on the other half of the AI-developer workflow!
  If the /wiki is a Confluence you can talk to, /task is the JIRA/Trello version:
  a backlog that lives next to the code as files, not in a SaaS.

  Same principles, different artifact:

  - **One self-contained markdown file per task**, written so a single-shot agent
    can pick it up and implement it with no chat context.
  - **Filesystem-native and git-tracked**: `tasks/` for open work, `tasks/archive/`
    for done/dropped, scope in the filename. No lock-in, diffs, and history for free.
  - **A bundled linter keeps it healthy** — naming, frontmatter, status↔location,
    links, dates (the task analogue of the wiki&#39;s lint pass).
  - **A small lifecycle of skills** instead of one mega-prompt:
    create → check → implement → audit → finish, plus a whole-tree fix/health pass.

  Open-source, MIT License, Multi-agent skill deployment:
  https://github.com/theafh/ai-modules/tree/main/plugins/ai_dev/skills" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
                Copy Markdown

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>        </details-menu>
      </details>
    </div>

    <div class="d-none d-sm-flex">
        

        

    </div>
  </div>

        <div class="edit-comment-hide">
  <task-lists disabled sortable>
    <div class="comment-body markdown-body js-comment-body soft-wrap user-select-contain d-block">
      <p dir="auto">This wiki pattern worked so well for <em>knowledge</em> that I tried the same<br>
plain-markdown-over-LLM idea on the other half of the AI-developer workflow!<br>
If the /wiki is a Confluence you can talk to, /task is the JIRA/Trello version:<br>
a backlog that lives next to the code as files, not in a SaaS.</p>
<p dir="auto">Same principles, different artifact:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><strong>One self-contained markdown file per task</strong>, written so a single-shot agent<br>
can pick it up and implement it with no chat context.</li>
<li><strong>Filesystem-native and git-tracked</strong>: <code class="notranslate">tasks/</code> for open work, <code class="notranslate">tasks/archive/</code><br>
for done/dropped, scope in the filename. No lock-in, diffs, and history for free.</li>
<li><strong>A bundled linter keeps it healthy</strong> — naming, frontmatter, status↔location,<br>
links, dates (the task analogue of the wiki's lint pass).</li>
<li><strong>A small lifecycle of skills</strong> instead of one mega-prompt:<br>
create → check → implement → audit → finish, plus a whole-tree fix/health pass.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Open-source, MIT License, Multi-agent skill deployment:<br>
<a href="https://github.com/theafh/ai-modules/tree/main/plugins/ai_dev/skills">https://github.com/theafh/ai-modules/tree/main/plugins/ai_dev/skills</a></p>
    </div>
  </task-lists>
  
</div>

  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="Been circling the same three-layer shape (raw → wiki → outputs, markdown, pre-compiled, wiki-linked) — but I came at it from a different angle: not &quot;knowledge base as a product,&quot; more &quot;a container for my own continuity&quot; that any model loads to stay in phase with me across sessions and machines.

The one place I deliberately diverge from your setup: I *don&#39;t* let it drift toward &quot;none written by me directly.&quot; The model writes the derivative / linking / consistency layer; the framing and the decisions stay authored by me — otherwise the continuity quietly stops being mine.

Which makes me wonder about something bigger than a second brain: if everyone runs a personal container like this and the model becomes the interchangeable part, the interesting unit isn&#39;t one general model anymore — it&#39;s (person × container × commodity intelligence). Feels less like &quot;AGI living in a model&quot; and more like generality relocating into millions of personal substrates. Curious whether you see it pointing that way too.

Open-sourced the bare structure if it&#39;s useful to anyone: https://github.com/hegu-1/personal-memory-vault-starter

        <div class="edit-comment-hide">
  <task-lists disabled sortable>
    <div class="comment-body markdown-body js-comment-body soft-wrap user-select-contain d-block">
      <p dir="auto">Been circling the same three-layer shape (raw → wiki → outputs, markdown, pre-compiled, wiki-linked) — but I came at it from a different angle: not "knowledge base as a product," more "a container for my own continuity" that any model loads to stay in phase with me across sessions and machines.</p>
<p dir="auto">The one place I deliberately diverge from your setup: I <em>don't</em> let it drift toward "none written by me directly." The model writes the derivative / linking / consistency layer; the framing and the decisions stay authored by me — otherwise the continuity quietly stops being mine.</p>
<p dir="auto">Which makes me wonder about something bigger than a second brain: if everyone runs a personal container like this and the model becomes the interchangeable part, the interesting unit isn't one general model anymore — it's (person × container × commodity intelligence). Feels less like "AGI living in a model" and more like generality relocating into millions of personal substrates. Curious whether you see it pointing that way too.</p>
<p dir="auto">Open-sourced the bare structure if it's useful to anyone: <a href="https://github.com/hegu-1/personal-memory-vault-starter">https://github.com/hegu-1/personal-memory-vault-starter</a></p>
    </div>
  </task-lists>
  
</div>

    <path d="M8 9a1.5 1.5 0 1 0 0-3 1.5 1.5 0 0 0 0 3ZM1.5 9a1.5 1.5 0 1 0 0-3 1.5 1.5 0 0 0 0 3Zm13 0a1.5 1.5 0 1 0 0-3 1.5 1.5 0 0 0 0 3Z"></path>

            <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy aria-label="Copy link" for="gistcomment-6176208-permalink" role="menuitem" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
              Copy link

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>              <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="**Architecture Discussion: Overcoming the &quot;Flat Markdown Mutation Problem&quot; in LLM-OS environments (Flat Files vs Outliner AST)** 

LOGSEQ (block granularity) vs Obsidian (page granularity) and my Matryca Plumber

Hi Andrej / everyone,

I’ve been heavily inspired by your concepts around the LLM OS and using flat Markdown as the filesystem for AI agents. I’ve been building autonomous loops using this exact paradigm, but I hit a massive architectural wall when using standard editors (like Obsidian) as the frontend.

The Problem: The &quot;Flat File Mutation&quot; Danger
When an AI agent tries to autonomously inject properties, update status tags, or surgically edit a document in the background while the user is typing, flat Markdown fails.

Standard LLMs struggle to parse the exact line numbers or preserve complex metadata in flat .md files without corrupting the surrounding text or triggering race conditions (causing data loss).

The Workaround/Experiment
I realized that for an LLM to truly act as a safe background daemon, the filesystem needs an inherently rigid structure. I pivoted the experiment to LOGSEQ (which stores data as a strict Block-based Outliner AST, with UUIDs per block) and implemented an Optimistic Concurrency Control (OCC) locking mechanism via mmap.

By doing this, the LLM doesn&#39;t have to guess where to inject data. It targets the block UUID.

1. The human types on the frontend.
2. The AI reads the AST, computes semantic tags, and injects properties in the background.
3. Zero file corruption.

I built an open-source Python daemon to prove this concept (matryca-plumber), fully protecting the AI-to-File boundary with strict token limits and JSON recovery. It has also an MCP and CLI to dialogue with notes and write directly to .MD files.

https://github.com/MarcoPorcellato/matryca-plumber

I wanted to share this architectural finding here because as we build out the &quot;LLM Wiki&quot; or &quot;LLM OS&quot; concepts, shifting the storage paradigm from Flat Text AST to Block Outliner AST seems to be the only way to grant LLMs safe, concurrent write-access to human notes.

Would love to hear your thoughts on concurrency and background mutations in your Obsidian setups!" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
                Copy Markdown

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>        </details-menu>
      </details>
    </div>

    <div class="d-none d-sm-flex">
        

        

    </div>
  </div>

  <h3 class="f5 text-normal py-2" style="flex: 1 1 auto">
    <div class="d-flex flex-items-center flex-wrap gap-1">
      <span class="d-inline-flex">
          
      </span>

      <strong>
            <a class="author Link--primary text-bold css-overflow-wrap-anywhere " show_full_name="false" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/MarcoPorcellato/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="/MarcoPorcellato">MarcoPorcellato</a>
  

      </strong>

      

      commented 


        <a href="/karpathy/442a6bf555914893e9891c11519de94f?permalink_comment_id=6176208#gistcomment-6176208" id="gistcomment-6176208-permalink" class="Link--secondary js-timestamp"><relative-time datetime="2026-05-31T06:39:48Z" class="no-wrap">May 31, 2026</relative-time></a>



      
    </div>

  </h3>

        <div class="edit-comment-hide">
  <task-lists disabled sortable>
    <div class="comment-body markdown-body js-comment-body soft-wrap user-select-contain d-block">
      <p dir="auto"><strong>Architecture Discussion: Overcoming the "Flat Markdown Mutation Problem" in LLM-OS environments (Flat Files vs Outliner AST)</strong></p>
<p dir="auto">LOGSEQ (block granularity) vs Obsidian (page granularity) and my Matryca Plumber</p>
<p dir="auto">Hi Andrej / everyone,</p>
<p dir="auto">I’ve been heavily inspired by your concepts around the LLM OS and using flat Markdown as the filesystem for AI agents. I’ve been building autonomous loops using this exact paradigm, but I hit a massive architectural wall when using standard editors (like Obsidian) as the frontend.</p>
<p dir="auto">The Problem: The "Flat File Mutation" Danger<br>
When an AI agent tries to autonomously inject properties, update status tags, or surgically edit a document in the background while the user is typing, flat Markdown fails.</p>
<p dir="auto">Standard LLMs struggle to parse the exact line numbers or preserve complex metadata in flat .md files without corrupting the surrounding text or triggering race conditions (causing data loss).</p>
<p dir="auto">The Workaround/Experiment<br>
I realized that for an LLM to truly act as a safe background daemon, the filesystem needs an inherently rigid structure. I pivoted the experiment to LOGSEQ (which stores data as a strict Block-based Outliner AST, with UUIDs per block) and implemented an Optimistic Concurrency Control (OCC) locking mechanism via mmap.</p>
<p dir="auto">By doing this, the LLM doesn't have to guess where to inject data. It targets the block UUID.</p>
<ol dir="auto">
<li>The human types on the frontend.</li>
<li>The AI reads the AST, computes semantic tags, and injects properties in the background.</li>
<li>Zero file corruption.</li>
</ol>
<p dir="auto">I built an open-source Python daemon to prove this concept (matryca-plumber), fully protecting the AI-to-File boundary with strict token limits and JSON recovery. It has also an MCP and CLI to dialogue with notes and write directly to .MD files.</p>
<p dir="auto"><a href="https://github.com/MarcoPorcellato/matryca-plumber">https://github.com/MarcoPorcellato/matryca-plumber</a></p>
<p dir="auto">I wanted to share this architectural finding here because as we build out the "LLM Wiki" or "LLM OS" concepts, shifting the storage paradigm from Flat Text AST to Block Outliner AST seems to be the only way to grant LLMs safe, concurrent write-access to human notes.</p>
<p dir="auto">Would love to hear your thoughts on concurrency and background mutations in your Obsidian setups!</p>
    </div>
  </task-lists>
  
</div>

            <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy aria-label="Copy link" for="gistcomment-6176649-permalink" role="menuitem" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
              Copy link

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>              <span data-view-component="true">
  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="Took this pattern in a security direction and wanted to share the concept in case it&#39;s useful to others here.

The thing that struck me: an autonomously-ingesting wiki is itself an indirect-prompt-injection surface. A crafted source can plant instructions that persist into the wiki and poison later sessions. The lint step in the original idea checks correctness and contradictions – but not adversariality. Those are orthogonal axes.

So I built a version hardened around one invariant: untrusted input (a source) must never reach a channel later treated as trusted (the wiki). Concretely:

- source text nonce-delimited and declared untrusted at every model boundary (extraction, review, read-time)
- an independent second model reviews each write for manipulation, not correctness (four-eyes)
- trust-tiering by host; weakest level propagates
- git-backed provenance per claim, so a poisoned entry is findable and revertible
- an injection corpus mapping each attack to the gate it must fail at, plus OWASP LLM Top 10 / MITRE ATLAS

It&#39;s defense-in-depth, not a guarantee – the sanitizer is heuristic and the reviewer is still an LLM. Code and the threat-model write-up:
https://github.com/NicoBleh/secure-llm-wiki" data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">
      
                Copy Markdown

</clipboard-copy>  <div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="sr-only" data-clipboard-copy-feedback></div>
</span>        </details-menu>
      </details>
    </div>

  <h3 class="f5 text-normal py-2" style="flex: 1 1 auto">
    <div class="d-flex flex-items-center flex-wrap gap-1">
      <span class="d-inline-flex">
          
      </span>

      <strong>
            <a class="author Link--primary text-bold css-overflow-wrap-anywhere " show_full_name="false" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/NicoBleh/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="/NicoBleh">NicoBleh</a>

        <a href="/karpathy/442a6bf555914893e9891c11519de94f?permalink_comment_id=6176649#gistcomment-6176649" id="gistcomment-6176649-permalink" class="Link--secondary js-timestamp"><relative-time datetime="2026-05-31T14:29:12Z" class="no-wrap">May 31, 2026</relative-time></a>



      
    </div>

  </h3>
</div>


        <div class="edit-comment-hide">
  <task-lists disabled sortable>
    <div class="comment-body markdown-body js-comment-body soft-wrap user-select-contain d-block">
      <p dir="auto">Took this pattern in a security direction and wanted to share the concept in case it's useful to others here.</p>
<p dir="auto">The thing that struck me: an autonomously-ingesting wiki is itself an indirect-prompt-injection surface. A crafted source can plant instructions that persist into the wiki and poison later sessions. The lint step in the original idea checks correctness and contradictions – but not adversariality. Those are orthogonal axes.</p>
<p dir="auto">So I built a version hardened around one invariant: untrusted input (a source) must never reach a channel later treated as trusted (the wiki). Concretely:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>source text nonce-delimited and declared untrusted at every model boundary (extraction, review, read-time)</li>
<li>an independent second model reviews each write for manipulation, not correctness (four-eyes)</li>
<li>trust-tiering by host; weakest level propagates</li>
<li>git-backed provenance per claim, so a poisoned entry is findable and revertible</li>
<li>an injection corpus mapping each attack to the gate it must fail at, plus OWASP LLM Top 10 / MITRE ATLAS</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">It's defense-in-depth, not a guarantee – the sanitizer is heuristic and the reviewer is still an LLM. Code and the threat-model write-up:<br>
<a href="https://github.com/NicoBleh/secure-llm-wiki">https://github.com/NicoBleh/secure-llm-wiki</a></p>
    </div>
  </task-lists>
  
</div>

  <clipboard-copy role="menuitem" value="I started doing exactly this a few months ago. Started with my company&#39;s &quot;sales and marketing&quot;, then my personal stuff, now every code repository. raw-data directory, steering files to process input into the knowledge base, all files reference each other to build a tree, linting rules, a concept of &quot;knowledge / archive&quot; vs. &quot;current state&quot; which can be either a brief of the most current action items, or the most recent reads on a bunch of things, or what I call &quot;initiatives&quot; which is: something with a start date, an end date, a goal to achieve and a way to measure it. Then there&#39;s the linting rules and a &quot;state&quot; file which is every single piece of raw-data ever processed, what documents it was distilled to, what was the original file name and a brief description. The state is committed, the raw-data isn&#39;t. Then since it&#39;s domain-specific knowledge-bases each has a few concepts and ontologies of stuff it needs to track in the documents and how they must be processed. So my workflow is usually dump a bunch of stuff in raw-data, tell the kb to eat it and it&#39;s done. It&#39;s got a rule so that sessions can&#39;t be load bearing, context is always ephemeral, everything must be crystallized unless it&#39;s really unimportant or noise." data-view-component="true" class="dropdown-item btn-link">

    <div class="d-flex flex-items-center flex-wrap gap-1">
      <span class="d-inline-flex">
          
      </span>

      <strong>
            <a class="author Link--primary text-bold css-overflow-wrap-anywhere " show_full_name="false" data-hovercard-type="user" data-hovercard-url="/users/vforvalerio87/hovercard" data-octo-click="hovercard-link-click" data-octo-dimensions="link_type:self" href="/vforvalerio87">vforvalerio87</a>

      commented 


        <a href="/karpathy/442a6bf555914893e9891c11519de94f?permalink_comment_id=6176989#gistcomment-6176989" id="gistcomment-6176989-permalink" class="Link--secondary js-timestamp"><relative-time datetime="2026-05-31T19:53:32Z" class="no-wrap">May 31, 2026</relative-time></a>



      
    </div>

  </h3>
</div>


        <div class="edit-comment-hide">
  <task-lists disabled sortable>
    <div class="comment-body markdown-body js-comment-body soft-wrap user-select-contain d-block">
      <p dir="auto">I started doing exactly this a few months ago. Started with my company's "sales and marketing", then my personal stuff, now every code repository. raw-data directory, steering files to process input into the knowledge base, all files reference each other to build a tree, linting rules, a concept of "knowledge / archive" vs. "current state" which can be either a brief of the most current action items, or the most recent reads on a bunch of things, or what I call "initiatives" which is: something with a start date, an end date, a goal to achieve and a way to measure it. Then there's the linting rules and a "state" file which is every single piece of raw-data ever processed, what documents it was distilled to, what was the original file name and a brief description. The state is committed, the raw-data isn't. Then since it's domain-specific knowledge-bases each has a few concepts and ontologies of stuff it needs to track in the documents and how they must be processed. So my workflow is usually dump a bunch of stuff in raw-data, tell the kb to eat it and it's done. It's got a rule so that sessions can't be load bearing, context is always ephemeral, everything must be crystallized unless it's really unimportant or noise.</p>
    </div>
  </task-lists>
  
</div>
